3:08:34 It's those little things that you don't
3:08:36 find in the documentation very easily,
3:08:38 but rather it's a best practice that you
3:08:41 have to dig through GitHub discussions
3:08:42 to figure it out fully. I zoomed out
3:08:44 this code so you get a better
3:08:46 understanding of the entire homepage
3:08:48 that we have right now. Notice a couple
3:08:50 of things. We have a single scroll view
3:08:52 right here where we said show vertical
3:08:55 scroll indicator is set to false. But
3:08:59 doesn't matter that we're hiding the
3:09:00 indicator. This scroll view is a
3:09:03 vertical view. And within that vertical
3:09:06 view, we also have another flat list.
3:09:10 This one right here, which renders all
3:09:12 the movie cards. Those movies are also
3:09:15 in a vertical view. And it is not
3:09:18 generally recommended to have two
3:09:20 virtualized lists or scroll views or
3:09:23 flat lists in the same orientation. See,
3:09:26 this flat list is not a problem. This
3:09:29 one is horizontal, and that's okay when
3:09:31 it comes to the performance. But having
3:09:33 two huge flat lists that are of the same
3:09:36 orientation is generally not considered
3:09:39 a good practice. We're using a scroll
3:09:42 view to render most of the movies. And
3:09:44 if you go to the docs, it's going to say
3:09:47 scroll view versus flat list. Which one
3:09:50 should you use? Well, scroll view
3:09:52 renders all its children components at
3:09:54 once, but this has a performance
3:09:58 downside. Imagine you have a very long
3:10:01 list of items you want to display, maybe
3:10:03 several screens worth of content.
3:10:05 Creating JS components and native views
3:10:08 for everything all at once, much of
3:10:10 which may not be even shown, will
3:10:12 contribute to slow rendering and
3:10:14 increased memory usage. And this is
3:10:16 where the flat list comes into play.
3:10:19 Because flat list is smart, it renders
3:10:22 items lazily just before they're about
3:10:24 to appear. And it removes the items that
3:10:27 scroll way off the screen to save memory
3:10:29 and processing time. So in other words,
3:10:32 when you have large lists of vertical
3:10:34 elements, you want to use a flat list
3:10:37 and not a scroll view. And in our case,
3:10:40 if you look at the flat list, we
3:10:42 technically didn't allow it to reach its
3:10:44 full potential because we have set
3:10:46 scroll enabled to false, which
3:10:48 technically means that we let the scroll
3:10:50 view do its job. Even though it is
3:10:53 totally fine, perfectly fine for our use
3:10:56 case as we don't have many elements and
3:10:58 we wouldn't even been able to harness
3:11:00 the power of the flat list for bigger
3:11:03 applications. It makes a big difference.
3:11:06 And that's exactly why I wanted to leave
3:11:08 this for the end of this video. While it
3:11:11 is totally fine for smaller apps, for
3:11:14 larger apps, you just got to be smarter
3:11:17 and utilize everything that the software
3:11:19 allows you to do. In this case, React
3:11:22 Native's built-in components, like the
3:11:24 flat list, are super powerful, but you

3:22:30 before we had to had a bridge that would
3:22:33 communicate between JavaScript and
3:22:35 native mobile application code. This

3:22:54 rendering, turbo modules for native
3:22:56 modules, and JSI to allow direct
3:22:59 synchronous communication between
3:23:00 JavaScript and native code without the

7:15:04 screen. Rather, you're only able to
7:15:07 scroll the flat list content. See how
7:15:10 the header remains there, but flat list
7:15:12 is scrollable. Why is that? Well, in web
7:15:16 development, the browser actually adds
7:15:18 scroll bars if the content exceeds the
7:15:21 viewport. But in React Native, you're
7:15:24 working in a native mobile environment
7:15:26 where the layout engine doesn't
7:15:28 necessarily assume that you want to
7:15:30 scroll. So you must explicitly wrap your
7:15:33 content in a scrollable container like a
7:15:35 scroll view or a flat list in this case.
7:15:39 If you want to add horizontal or
7:15:41 vertical scrolling behavior like we have
7:15:43 here. So in case you want to make the
7:15:45 whole thing scrollable, you can just
7:15:47 wrap it in a scroll view which you can
7:15:51 import from React Native
7:15:54 and make sure to properly close it right
7:15:56 at the bottom right inside of the safe
7:15:59 area view. If you do that, you can
7:16:02 notice that now the whole thing is
7:16:04 scrollable. But with this in mind, we
7:16:07 have one tiny problem. And that is that
7:16:09 if you open up your application
7:16:11 terminal, you'll see that it says that
7:16:13 virtualized lists should never be nested
7:16:16 inside plain scroll views with the same
7:16:18 orientation because it can break
7:16:20 windowing and other functionality. Use
7:16:23 another virtualized listbacked container
7:16:25 instead. This problem is happening
7:16:28 because a flat list which is a
7:16:30 virtualized list is designed to render
7:16:32 only the visible items for performance,
7:16:34 not the ones that cannot fit on the
7:16:36 screen. Wrapping it in a scroll view
7:16:39 though, which tries to render everything
7:16:41 at once, disables that optimization,
7:16:44 causing poor performance, layout
7:16:46 glitches, and broken scroll behavior. It
7:16:49 is a pattern that you don't want to do
7:16:51 in React Native. So, please never ever
7:16:54 wrap a flat list or any other
7:16:56 virtualized list inside of a scroll view
7:16:59 ever again because that's a React Native

9:04:04 them. And let's copy the customizations
9:04:07 collection ID and paste it over right
9:04:09 here. Customizations
9:04:12 collection ID and we can paste it right
9:04:16 here. Great. So, you might think that we
9:04:20 have everything we need, but there's one
9:04:23 challenge here. Some customizations
9:04:26 apply only to burgers, but not to
9:04:28 pizzas, for example. Others apply to
9:04:31 both. This is a classic many to many
9:04:34 relationship. A menu item can have many
9:04:37 customizations and a customization can
9:04:39 belong to many menu items. And in
9:04:42 relational modeling, the cleanest way to
9:04:44 handle this is by introducing a join
9:04:47 table or in Apprite's case, a new
9:04:50 collection. This collection will be
9:04:52 called menu
9:04:54 customizations
9:04:56 and is essentially a joint table. It'll
9:04:59 contain pairs of references, one
9:05:02 pointing to the menu item and the other
9:05:04 to the customization. This architecture
9:05:06 will keep the data clean, reusable, and
9:05:10 consistent.
9:05:11 For example, a user might add a
9:05:14 pepperoni pizza with extra cheese and a
9:05:16 Coke to the cart. And because these
9:05:18 customizations exist as separate
9:05:20 documents, the app doesn't duplicate
9:05:22 that information. It simply links them.
9:05:25 I mean, you could say that you could
9:05:26 embed customizations directly within the
9:05:28 menu items that we create in our
9:05:30 database. It's a tempting shortcut, but
9:05:33 it quickly falls apart as the app grows
9:05:36 because embedding customization makes it
9:05:38 hard to update prices globally, leads to
9:05:41 inconsistent naming, and makes filtering
9:05:43 or analytics harder. By normalizing
9:05:46 data, separating it into reusable
9:05:48 collections. This structure is more
9:05:50 scalable and maintainable.
9:05:53 There's also flexibility built into this
9:05:55 design. Customizations are optional, and
9:05:57 not every item needs to have a topping
9:05:59 or a side. So even if a menu item has no
9:06:02 customizations, it can still be stored,
9:06:05 displayed, and priced properly. This
9:06:07 design supports both simple and complex
9:06:10 items equally. And this is just one of
9:06:12 the advanced database optimizations that
9:06:14 we teach over on jsmastery.com.
9:06:17 This one specifically, I believe I cover
9:06:19 within the ultimate nex.js course where
9:06:22 we try to do something similar with the
